<p><b>Munich</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/juː/: 'u' in 'cute'">juː</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span></span>/</a></span></span>; German: <span lang="de"><i>München</i></span>, <small>pronounced</small> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German" title="Help:IPA/Standard German">[ˈmʏnçn̩]</a></span><small class="nowrap metadata">&#160;(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:De-M%C3%BCnchen.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cc/De-M%C3%BCnchen.ogg" class="internal" title="De-München.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small>,<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bavarian_language" title="Bavarian language">Bavarian</a>: <span lang="bar"><i>Minga</i></span> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ˈmɪŋ(ː)ɐ]</span>, <a href="/wiki/Czech_language" title="Czech language">Czech</a>: <span lang="cs"><i>Mnichov</i></span>) is the capital and the most populated city in the <a href="/wiki/States_of_Germany" title="States of Germany">German state</a> of <a href="/wiki/Bavaria" title="Bavaria">Bavaria</a>, on the banks of River <a href="/wiki/Isar" title="Isar">Isar</a> north of the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Limestone_Alps" title="Northern Limestone Alps">Bavarian Alps</a>. Munich is also the <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_in_Germany_by_population" title="List of cities in Germany by population">third largest city</a> in Germany, after <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a>, and the 12th biggest city of the European Union, with a population of around 1.5&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Munich_Metropolitan_Region" title="Munich Metropolitan Region">Munich Metropolitan Region</a> is home to 6 million people.<sup id="cite_ref-mmr_official_site_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mmr_official_site-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Munich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The city is a major centre of art, advanced technologies, finance, publishing, culture, innovation, education, business, and tourism in Germany and Europe and enjoys a very high standard and quality of living, reaching first in Germany and fourth worldwide according to the <a href="/wiki/Mercer_Quality_of_Living_Survey" title="Mercer Quality of Living Survey">2015 Mercer survey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Globalization_and_World_Cities_Research_Network" title="Globalization and World Cities Research Network">Globalization and World Rankings Research Institute</a> Munich is considered an <a href="/wiki/Global_city" title="Global city">alpha-world city</a>, as of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Munich&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>The name of the city is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">Old</a>/<a href="/wiki/Middle_High_German" title="Middle High German">Middle High German</a> term <i>Munichen</i>, meaning "by the monks", which in turn is derived from Mönch (which in the end derives from ancient Greek μοναχός). It derives from the monks of the <a href="/wiki/Benedictine_order" class="mw-redirect" title="Benedictine order">Benedictine order</a>, who ran a monastery at the place that was later to become the Old Town of Munich; hence the monk depicted on <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms_of_Munich" title="Coat of arms of Munich">the city's coat of arms</a>. Munich was first mentioned in 1158. From 1255 the city was seat of the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Bavaria" title="Duchy of Bavaria">Bavarian Dukes</a>. Black and gold – the colours of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> – have been the city's official colours since the time of <a href="/wiki/Louis_IV,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Louis IV, Holy Roman Emperor">Ludwig the Bavarian</a>, when it was an imperial residence. Following a final reunification of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Wittelsbach" title="House of Wittelsbach">Wittelsbachian</a> Duchy of Bavaria, previously divided and sub-divided for more than 200 years, the town became the country's sole capital in 1506.</p>
<p>Catholic Munich was a cultural stronghold of the <a href="/wiki/Counter-Reformation" title="Counter-Reformation">Counter-Reformation</a> and a political point of divergence during the resulting <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years' War">Thirty Years' War</a>, but remained physically untouched despite an occupation by the Protestant <a href="/wiki/Swedes" title="Swedes">Swedes</a>; as the townsfolk would rather open the gates of their town than risk siege and almost inevitable destruction.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Like wide parts of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>, the area recovered slowly economically.</p>
<p>Having evolved from a duchy's capital into that of an <a href="/wiki/Prince-elector" title="Prince-elector">electorate</a> (1623), and later a sovereign <a href="/wiki/Monarchy" title="Monarchy">kingdom</a> (1806), Munich has been a major European centre of arts, architecture, culture and science since the early 19th century, heavily sponsored by the Bavarian monarchs. In 1918, during the <a href="/wiki/German_Revolution_of_1918%E2%80%9319" title="German Revolution of 1918–19">German Revolution</a>, the ruling <a href="/wiki/House_of_Wittelsbach" title="House of Wittelsbach">house of Wittelsbach</a>, which governed Bavaria since 1180, was forced to abdicate in Munich and a short-lived <a href="/wiki/Bavarian_Soviet_Republic" title="Bavarian Soviet Republic">socialist republic</a> was declared.</p>
<p>In the 1920s, Munich became home to several political factions, among them the <a href="/wiki/NSDAP" class="mw-redirect" title="NSDAP">NSDAP</a>, which was founded in the city in 1920. Though the first attempt of the Nazi movement to take over the German government in 1923 with the <a href="/wiki/Feldherrnhalle" title="Feldherrnhalle">Beer Hall Putsch</a>, which was stopped by the Bavarian police in Munich with gunfire, the Nazis declared the city after their rise to power to be <i>Hauptstadt der Bewegung</i> (lit.: "Capital of the movement"). During World War II, Munich was heavily bombed and more than 50% of the entire city and up to 90% of the historic centre were destroyed.</p>
<p>The postwar period until 1949 was characterised by American occupation; after 1949, there was a great increase in population and economic power during the years of <i><a href="/wiki/Wirtschaftswunder" title="Wirtschaftswunder">Wirtschaftswunder</a></i> (lit.: "economic miracle"). Unlike many other German cities which were heavily bombed and destroyed during World War II, Munich rebuilt most of its traditional cityscape and added a modern face with the <a href="/wiki/Olympiastadion_(Munich)" title="Olympiastadion (Munich)">Olympic Stadium</a>, while hosting the <a href="/wiki/1972_Summer_Olympics" title="1972 Summer Olympics">1972 Summer Olympics</a>. Especially since the 1980s, Munich and the surrounding region were characterised by strong economic growth, high-tech industries and scientific institutions, very low unemployment rates and a strong influx of people. The city is home to major corporations like <a href="/wiki/BMW" title="BMW">BMW</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siemens" title="Siemens">Siemens</a>, <a href="/wiki/MAN_SE" title="MAN SE">MAN</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Linde_Group" title="The Linde Group">Linde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Allianz_SE" class="mw-redirect" title="Allianz SE">Allianz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Munich_Re" title="Munich Re">MunichRE</a>, as well as many small and medium-sized companies.</p>
<p>Munich is home to many national and international authorities, major universities, major museums and theatres. Its numerous architectural attractions, international sports events, exhibitions and conferences and its annual <a href="/wiki/Oktoberfest" title="Oktoberfest">Oktoberfest</a> attract considerable <a href="/wiki/Tourism" title="Tourism">tourism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Munich is one of the most prosperous and fastest growing cities in Germany. It is a top-ranked destination for migration and expatriate location, despite being the municipality with the highest population density in Germany (4,500 people per km²) . Munich hosts more than 530,000 people of foreign background, making up 37.7% of its population.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Munich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Munich" title="History of Munich">History of Munich</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Munich" title="Timeline of Munich">Timeline of Munich</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Germany_M%C3%BCnchen_Monks.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Germany_M%C3%BCnchen_Monks.jpg/220px-Germany_M%C3%BCnchen_Monks.jpg" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Germany_M%C3%BCnchen_Monks.jpg/330px-Germany_M%C3%BCnchen_Monks.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Germany_M%C3%BCnchen_Monks.jpg/440px-Germany_M%C3%BCnchen_Monks.jpg 2x" data-file-width="496" data-file-height="376" /></a>

<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Germany_M%C3%BCnchen_Monks.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Munich city <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms_of_Munich" title="Coat of arms of Munich">coat of arms</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_as_medieval_town">Origin as medieval town</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Munich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Origin as medieval town">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stadtansicht_1572.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Stadtansicht_1572.jpg/230px-Stadtansicht_1572.jpg" width="230" height="97" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Stadtansicht_1572.jpg/345px-Stadtansicht_1572.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Stadtansicht_1572.jpg/460px-Stadtansicht_1572.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2425" data-file-height="1019" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stadtansicht_1572.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Munich in the 16th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first known settlement in the area was of <a href="/wiki/Benedictine" class="mw-redirect" title="Benedictine">Benedictine</a> monks on the <a href="/wiki/Old_Salt_Route" title="Old Salt Route">Old Salt Route</a>. The foundation date is considered the year 1158, the date the city was first mentioned in a document. The document was signed in <a href="/wiki/Augsburg" title="Augsburg">Augsburg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> By then, the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Welf" title="House of Welf">Guelph</a> <a href="/wiki/Henry_the_Lion" title="Henry the Lion">Henry the Lion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Saxony" title="Duchy of Saxony">Duke of Saxony</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_rulers_of_Bavaria" title="List of rulers of Bavaria">Bavaria</a>, had built a toll bridge over the river Isar next to the monk settlement and on the salt route.</p>
<p>In 1175, Munich received city status and fortification. In 1180, with the trial of Henry the Lion, <a href="/wiki/Otto_I_of_Wittelsbach,_Duke_of_Bavaria" class="mw-redirect" title="Otto I of Wittelsbach, Duke of Bavaria">Otto I Wittelsbach</a> became Duke of Bavaria, and Munich was handed to the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Archdiocese_of_Munich_and_Freising" title="Roman Catholic Archdiocese of Munich and Freising">Bishop of Freising</a>. (Wittelsbach's heirs, the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Wittelsbach" title="House of Wittelsbach">Wittelsbach dynasty</a>, ruled Bavaria until 1918.) In 1240, Munich was transferred to <a href="/wiki/Otto_II_Wittelsbach,_Duke_of_Bavaria" class="mw-redirect" title="Otto II Wittelsbach, Duke of Bavaria">Otto II Wittelsbach</a> and in 1255, when the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Bavaria" title="Duchy of Bavaria">Duchy of Bavaria</a> was split in two, Munich became the ducal residence of <a href="/wiki/Upper_Bavaria" title="Upper Bavaria">Upper Bavaria</a>.</p>
<p>Duke <a href="/wiki/Louis_IV,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Louis IV, Holy Roman Emperor">Louis IV</a>, a native of Munich, was elected German king in 1314 and crowned as <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Holy Roman Emperor</a> in 1328. He strengthened the city's position by granting it the salt monopoly, thus assuring it of additional income. In the late 15th century, Munich underwent a revival of <a href="/wiki/Gothic_art" title="Gothic art">gothic arts</a>: the Old Town Hall was enlarged, and Munich's largest <a href="/wiki/Gothic_architecture" title="Gothic architecture">gothic</a> church – the <a href="/wiki/Munich_Frauenkirche" title="Munich Frauenkirche">Frauenkirche</a> – now a cathedral, was constructed in only 20 years, starting in 1468.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Capital_of_reunited_Bavaria">Capital of reunited Bavaria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Munich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Capital of reunited Bavaria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mun_flags_frauenkirche.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Mun_flags_frauenkirche.jpg/170px-Mun_flags_frauenkirche.jpg" width="170" height="252" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Mun_flags_frauenkirche.jpg/255px-Mun_flags_frauenkirche.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Mun_flags_frauenkirche.jpg/340px-Mun_flags_frauenkirche.jpg 2x" data-file-width="378" data-file-height="560" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mun_flags_frauenkirche.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Banners with the colours of Munich (left) and Bavaria (right) with the <a href="/wiki/Munich_Frauenkirche" title="Munich Frauenkirche">Frauenkirche</a> in the background</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When Bavaria was reunited in 1506, Munich became its capital. The arts and politics became increasingly influenced by the court (see <a href="/wiki/Orlande_de_Lassus" title="Orlande de Lassus">Orlando di Lasso</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Sch%C3%BCtz" title="Heinrich Schütz">Heinrich Schütz</a> and later <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart" title="Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart">Mozart</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wagner" title="Richard Wagner">Richard Wagner</a>). During the 16th century, Munich was a centre of the German <a href="/wiki/Counter-Reformation" title="Counter-Reformation">counter reformation</a>, and also of <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">renaissance</a> arts. Duke <a href="/wiki/William_V,_Duke_of_Bavaria" title="William V, Duke of Bavaria">Wilhelm V</a> commissioned the Jesuit <a href="/wiki/St._Michael%27s_Church,_Munich" title="St. Michael's Church, Munich">Michaelskirche</a>, which became a centre for the counter-reformation, and also built the <a href="/wiki/Hofbr%C3%A4uhaus" class="mw-redirect" title="Hofbräuhaus">Hofbräuhaus</a> for brewing brown beer in 1589.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Catholic_League_(German)" title="Catholic League (German)">Catholic League</a> was founded in Munich in 1609.</p>
<p>In 1623, during the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years' War">Thirty Years' War</a>, Munich became electoral residence when <a href="/wiki/Maximilian_I,_Elector_of_Bavaria" title="Maximilian I, Elector of Bavaria">Maximilian I, Duke of Bavaria</a> was invested with the <a href="/wiki/Prince-elector" title="Prince-elector">electoral dignity</a>, but in 1632 the city was occupied by <a href="/wiki/Gustavus_Adolphus_of_Sweden" title="Gustavus Adolphus of Sweden">Gustav II Adolph of Sweden</a>. When the <a href="/wiki/Bubonic_plague" title="Bubonic plague">bubonic plague</a> broke out in 1634 and 1635, about one third of the population died. Under the regency of the Bavarian electors, Munich was an important centre of baroque life, but also had to suffer under <a href="/wiki/House_of_Habsburg" title="House of Habsburg">Habsburg</a> occupations in 1704 and 1742.</p>
<p>In 1806, the city became the capital of the new <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Bavaria" title="Kingdom of Bavaria">Kingdom of Bavaria</a>, with the state's parliament (the <i><a href="/wiki/Landtag" title="Landtag">Landtag</a>)</i> and the new <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Archdiocese_of_Munich_and_Freising" title="Roman Catholic Archdiocese of Munich and Freising">archdiocese of Munich and Freising</a> being located in the city. Twenty years later, <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Maximilian_University_of_Munich" title="Ludwig Maximilian University of Munich">Landshut University</a> was moved to Munich. Many of the city's finest buildings belong to this period and were built under the first three Bavarian kings. Especially <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_I_of_Bavaria" title="Ludwig I of Bavaria">Ludwig I</a> rendered outstanding services to Munich's status as a centre of the arts, attracting numerous artists and enhancing the city's architectural substance with grand boulevards and buildings. On the other hand, <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_II_of_Bavaria" title="Ludwig II of Bavaria">Ludwig II</a>, famous the world over as the fairytale king, was mostly aloof from his capital and focused more on his fanciful castles in the Bavarian countryside. Nevertheless, his patronage of Richard Wagner secured his posthumous reputation, as do his castles, which generate significant tourist income for Bavaria to this day. Later, Prince Regent <a href="/wiki/Luitpold,_Prince_Regent_of_Bavaria" title="Luitpold, Prince Regent of Bavaria">Luitpold's</a> years as regent were marked by tremendous artistic and cultural activity in Munich, enhancing its status as a cultural force of global importance (see <a href="/wiki/Franz_Stuck" title="Franz Stuck">Franz von Stuck</a> and <a href="/wiki/Der_Blaue_Reiter" title="Der Blaue Reiter">Der Blaue Reiter</a>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_I_to_World_War_II">World War I to World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Munich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: World War I to World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Following the outbreak of World War I in 1914, life in Munich became very difficult, as the Allied blockade of Germany led to food and fuel shortages. During French air raids in 1916, three bombs fell on Munich.</p>
<p>After World War I, the city was at the centre of much political unrest. In November 1918 on the eve of German revolution, <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_III_of_Bavaria" title="Ludwig III of Bavaria">Ludwig III</a> and his family fled the city. After the murder of the first republican <a href="/wiki/List_of_Ministers-President_of_Bavaria" title="List of Ministers-President of Bavaria">premier of Bavaria</a> <a href="/wiki/Kurt_Eisner" title="Kurt Eisner">Kurt Eisner</a> in February 1919 by <a href="/wiki/Anton_Graf_von_Arco_auf_Valley" title="Anton Graf von Arco auf Valley">Anton Graf von Arco auf Valley</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bavarian_Soviet_Republic" title="Bavarian Soviet Republic">Bavarian Soviet Republic</a> was proclaimed. When Communists took power, <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Lenin</a>, who had lived in Munich some years before, sent a congratulatory telegram, but the Soviet Republic was put down on 3 May 1919 by the <a href="/wiki/Freikorps" title="Freikorps">Freikorps</a>. While the republican government had been restored, Munich became a hotbed of extremist politics, among which <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">National Socialists</a> rose to prominence.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wardamage2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Wardamage2.jpg/220px-Wardamage2.jpg" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Wardamage2.jpg/330px-Wardamage2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Wardamage2.jpg/440px-Wardamage2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="617" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wardamage2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bombing damage to the Altstadt. Note the roofless and pockmarked Altes Rathaus looking up the Tal. The roofless Heilig-Geist-Kirche is on the right of the photo. Its spire, without the copper top, is behind the church. The Talbruck gate tower is missing completely.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1923, Adolf Hitler and his supporters, who were concentrated in Munich, staged the <a href="/wiki/Beer_Hall_Putsch" title="Beer Hall Putsch">Beer Hall Putsch</a>, an attempt to overthrow the <a href="/wiki/Weimar_Republic" title="Weimar Republic">Weimar Republic</a> and seize power. The revolt failed, resulting in Hitler's arrest and the temporary crippling of the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Party" title="Nazi Party">Nazi Party</a> (NSDAP), which was virtually unknown outside Munich. The city again became a Nazi stronghold when the party took power in Germany in 1933. The party created its first <a href="/wiki/Nazi_concentration_camp" class="mw-redirect" title="Nazi concentration camp">concentration camp</a> at <a href="/wiki/Dachau_concentration_camp" title="Dachau concentration camp">Dachau</a>, 16 kilometres (9.9 miles) north-west of the city. Because of its importance to the rise of National Socialism, Munich was referred to as the <i>Hauptstadt der Bewegung</i> ("Capital of the Movement"). The NSDAP headquarters were in Munich and many <i>Führerbauten</i> ("<i>Führer</i>-buildings") were built around the <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6nigsplatz,_Munich" title="Königsplatz, Munich">Königsplatz</a>, some of which still survive.</p>
<p>The city is known as the site of the culmination of the policy of <a href="/wiki/Munich_Agreement" title="Munich Agreement">appeasement</a> by Britain and France leading up to World War II. It was in Munich that British Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Neville_Chamberlain" title="Neville Chamberlain">Neville Chamberlain</a> assented to the annexation of Czechoslovakia's Sudetenland region into Greater Germany in the hopes of sating the desires of Hitler's <a href="/wiki/Third_Reich" class="mw-redirect" title="Third Reich">Third Reich</a>.</p>
<p>Munich was the base of the <a href="/wiki/White_Rose" title="White Rose">White Rose</a>, a student <a href="/wiki/Resistance_movement" title="Resistance movement">resistance movement</a> from June 1942 to February 1943. The core members were arrested and executed following a distribution of leaflets in <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Maximilian_University_of_Munich" title="Ludwig Maximilian University of Munich">Munich University</a> by <a href="/wiki/White_Rose" title="White Rose">Hans and Sophie Scholl</a>.</p>
<p>The city was heavily damaged by allied bombing during World War II – it was hit by 71 air raids during five years.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Postwar">Postwar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Munich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Postwar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BMW_Welt_Night.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/BMW_Welt_Night.jpg/220px-BMW_Welt_Night.jpg" width="220" height="125" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/BMW_Welt_Night.jpg/330px-BMW_Welt_Night.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/BMW_Welt_Night.jpg/440px-BMW_Welt_Night.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1700" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BMW_Welt_Night.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/BMW_Welt" title="BMW Welt">BMW Welt</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After US occupation in 1945, Munich was completely rebuilt following a meticulous and – by comparison to other war-ravaged <a href="/wiki/West_German" class="mw-redirect" title="West German">West German</a> cities<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> – rather conservative plan which preserved its pre-war street grid. In 1957, Munich's population surpassed 1 million. The city continued to play a highly significant role in the German economy, politics and culture, giving rise to its nickname <i>Heimliche Hauptstadt</i> ("secret capital") in the decades after World War II.</p>
<p>Munich was the site of the <a href="/wiki/1972_Summer_Olympics" title="1972 Summer Olympics">1972 Summer Olympics</a>, during which <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israeli</a> athletes were assassinated by <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_Fedayeen" class="mw-redirect" title="Palestinian Fedayeen">Palestinian fedayeen</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Munich_massacre" title="Munich massacre">Munich massacre</a>, when gunmen from the Palestinian "<a href="/wiki/Black_September_(group)" class="mw-redirect" title="Black September (group)">Black September</a>" group took hostage members of the Israeli Olympic team.</p>
<p>Most Munich residents enjoy a high quality of life. Mercer HR Consulting consistently rates the city among the top 10 cities with the highest quality of life worldwide – a 2011 survey ranked Munich as 4th.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The same company also ranks Munich as the 39th most expensive in the world and city most expensive major city in Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Munich enjoys a thriving economy, driven by the information technology, biotechnology, and publishing sectors. Environmental pollution is low, although as of 2006<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Munich&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> the city council is concerned about levels of <a href="/wiki/Particulate_matter" class="mw-redirect" title="Particulate matter">particulate matter</a> (PM), especially along the city's major thoroughfares. Since the enactment of <a href="/wiki/Particulate#EU_legislation" class="mw-redirect" title="Particulate">EU legislation concerning the concentration of particulate</a> in the air, environmental groups such as <a href="/wiki/Greenpeace" title="Greenpeace">Greenpeace</a> have staged large protest rallies to urge the city council and the State government to take a harder stance on pollution.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Today, the crime rate is low compared with other large German cities, such as <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a> or <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a>. For its high quality of life and safety, the city has been nicknamed "Toytown"<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> among the English-speaking residents. German inhabitants call it "Millionendorf", an expression which means "village of a million people". Due to the high standard of living in and the thriving economy of the city and the region, there was an influx of people and Munich's population surpassed 1.5 million by June 2015, an increase of more than 20% in 10 years.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Munich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stadtbild_M%C3%BCnchen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Stadtbild_M%C3%BCnchen.jpg/220px-Stadtbild_M%C3%BCnchen.jpg" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Stadtbild_M%C3%BCnchen.jpg/330px-Stadtbild_M%C3%BCnchen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Stadtbild_M%C3%BCnchen.jpg/440px-Stadtbild_M%C3%BCnchen.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4861" data-file-height="3285" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stadtbild_M%C3%BCnchen.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The inner city (2013).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Englischer_Garten_from_Monopteros.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Englischer_Garten_from_Monopteros.JPG/220px-Englischer_Garten_from_Monopteros.JPG" width="220" height="105" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Englischer_Garten_from_Monopteros.JPG/330px-Englischer_Garten_from_Monopteros.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Englischer_Garten_from_Monopteros.JPG/440px-Englischer_Garten_from_Monopteros.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="1467" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Englischer_Garten_from_Monopteros.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Munich: View from the <a href="/wiki/Englischer_Garten" title="Englischer Garten">Englischer Garten</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Munich lies on the elevated plains of <a href="/wiki/Upper_Bavaria" title="Upper Bavaria">Upper Bavaria</a>, about 50&#160;km (31.07&#160;mi) north of the northern edge of the <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a>, at an altitude of about 520&#160;m (1,706.04&#160;ft) <a href="/wiki/Above_mean_sea_level" class="mw-redirect" title="Above mean sea level">ASL</a>. The local rivers are the <a href="/wiki/Isar" title="Isar">Isar</a> and the <a href="/wiki/W%C3%BCrm" title="Würm">Würm</a>. Munich is situated in the Northern <a href="/wiki/Foothills" title="Foothills">Alpine Foreland</a>. The northern part of this sandy plateau includes a highly fertile <a href="/wiki/Flint" title="Flint">flint</a> area which is no longer affected by the <a href="/wiki/Fold_(geology)" title="Fold (geology)">folding</a> processes found in the Alps, while the southern part is covered with <a href="/wiki/Moraine" title="Moraine">morainic</a> hills. Between these are fields of <a href="/wiki/Fluvio-glacial" title="Fluvio-glacial">fluvio-glacial</a> out-wash, such as around Munich. Wherever these deposits get thinner, the <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">ground water</a> can permeate the gravel surface and flood the area, leading to <a href="/wiki/Marsh" title="Marsh">marshes</a> as in the north of Munich.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Munich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Munich's city climate lies between the <a href="/wiki/Humid_continental_climate" title="Humid continental climate">humid continental climate</a> (<a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen classification</a>: Dfb) and the <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_climate" title="Oceanic climate">oceanic climate</a> (<a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen classification</a>: Cfb).</p>
<p>The city centre lies between both climates, while the <a href="/wiki/Munich_Airport" title="Munich Airport">airport of Munich</a> has a <a href="/wiki/Humid_continental_climate" title="Humid continental climate">humid continental climate</a>. The warmest month, on average, is July. The coolest is January.</p>
<p>Showers and thunderstorms bring the highest average monthly precipitation in late spring and throughout the summer. The most precipitation occurs in June, on average. Winter tends to have less precipitation, the least in February.</p>
<p>The higher elevation and proximity to the Alps cause the city to have more rain and snow than many other parts of Germany. The Alps affect the city's climate in other ways too; for example, the warm downhill wind from the Alps (<a href="/wiki/F%C3%B6hn_wind" class="mw-redirect" title="Föhn wind">föhn wind</a>), which can raise temperatures sharply within a few hours even in the winter.</p>
<p>Being at the centre of Europe, Munich is subject to many climatic influences, so that weather conditions there are more variable than in other European cities, especially those further west and south of the Alps.</p>
<p>At Munich's official weather station, the highest and lowest temperatures ever measured are 37&#160;°C (99&#160;°F), on 13 August 2003, and −31.6&#160;°C (−24.9&#160;°F), on 12 February 1929.</p>

<p>From only 24,000 inhabitants in 1700, the city population doubled about every 30 years. It was 100,000 in 1852, 250,000 in 1883 and 500,000 in 1901. Since then, Munich has become Germany's third largest city. In 1933, 840,901 inhabitants were counted, and in 1957 over 1 million.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Immigration">Immigration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Munich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Immigration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" width="20" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31" /></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span"><b>This section needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Munich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small> <small><i>(June 2017)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>In July 2017, Munich had 1.42&#160;million inhabitants; 300,129 of those did not have <a href="/wiki/German_nationality_law" title="German nationality law">German citizenship</a>. The city has strong <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkan</a> communities. The largest groups of foreign nationals were <a href="/wiki/Turkish_people" title="Turkish people">Turks</a> (43,309), <a href="/wiki/Albanians" title="Albanians">Albanians</a> (30,385), <a href="/wiki/Croats" title="Croats">Croats</a> (24,866), <a href="/wiki/Serbs" title="Serbs">Serbs</a> (24,439), <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greeks</a> (22,486), <a href="/wiki/Austrians" title="Austrians">Austrians</a> (21,411), and <a href="/wiki/Italian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Italian people">Italians</a> (20,847). 37% of foreign nationals come from the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Munich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>About 45% of Munich's residents are not affiliated with any religious group, and this ratio represents the fastest growing segment of the population. As in the rest of Germany, the Roman Catholic and Protestant churches have experienced a continuous decline in membership. As of 31 December 2016, 32.4% of the city's inhabitants were <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a>, 11.6% <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestant</a> and 0.3% <a href="/wiki/Jewish" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewish">Jewish</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> In 2011, 7.5% were Muslim migrants from 21 countries of origin.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> About 3% adhere to other Christian denominations. There is also a small Old Catholic parish and an English-speaking parish of the <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_Church_(United_States)" title="Episcopal Church (United States)">Episcopal Church</a> in the city.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Munich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Munich's current mayor is <a href="/wiki/Dieter_Reiter" title="Dieter Reiter">Dieter Reiter</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_of_Germany" title="Social Democratic Party of Germany">Social Democratic Party of Germany</a>. Munich has been governed by the SPD for all but six years since 1948. This is remarkable because Bavaria – and particularly southern Bavaria – has long been a conservative stronghold, with the <a href="/wiki/Christian_Social_Union_of_Bavaria" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian Social Union of Bavaria">Christian Social Union</a> winning <a href="/wiki/Absolute_majority" class="mw-redirect" title="Absolute majority">absolute majorities</a> among the Bavarian electorate in many elections at the communal, state, and federal levels, and leading the Bavarian state government for all but three years since 1946. Bavaria's second most populous city, <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg" title="Nuremberg">Nuremberg</a>, is also one of the very few Bavarian cities governed by a SPD-led coalition.</p>
<p>As the capital of the Free State of Bavaria, Munich is an important political centre in Germany and the seat of the <a href="/wiki/Landtag_of_Bavaria" title="Landtag of Bavaria">Bavarian State Parliament</a>, the Staatskanzlei (the State Chancellery) and of all state departments.</p>
<p>Several national and international authorities are located in Munich, including the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Finance_Court_of_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="Federal Finance Court of Germany">Federal Finance Court of Germany</a> and the <a href="/wiki/European_Patent_Office" title="European Patent Office">European Patent Office</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Subdivisions">Subdivisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Munich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Subdivisions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Boroughs_of_Munich" title="Boroughs of Munich">Boroughs of Munich</a></div>
<p>Since the administrative reform in 1992, Munich is divided into 25 <a href="/wiki/Borough" title="Borough">boroughs</a> or <i>Stadtbezirke</i>, which themselves consist of sometimes quite distinct smaller quarters.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stadtbezirke_Lage_in_M%C3%BCnchen.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Stadtbezirke_Lage_in_M%C3%BCnchen.png/200px-Stadtbezirke_Lage_in_M%C3%BCnchen.png" width="200" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Stadtbezirke_Lage_in_M%C3%BCnchen.png/300px-Stadtbezirke_Lage_in_M%C3%BCnchen.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Stadtbezirke_Lage_in_M%C3%BCnchen.png/400px-Stadtbezirke_Lage_in_M%C3%BCnchen.png 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="378" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stadtbezirke_Lage_in_M%C3%BCnchen.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Munich's Boroughs</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Allach-Untermenzing" title="Allach-Untermenzing">Allach-Untermenzing</a> (23), <a href="/wiki/Altstadt-Lehel" title="Altstadt-Lehel">Altstadt-Lehel</a> (1), <a href="/wiki/Aubing-Lochhausen-Langwied" title="Aubing-Lochhausen-Langwied">Aubing-Lochhausen-Langwied</a> (22), <a href="/wiki/Au-Haidhausen" title="Au-Haidhausen">Au-Haidhausen</a> (5), <a href="/wiki/Berg_am_Laim" title="Berg am Laim">Berg am Laim</a> (14), <a href="/wiki/Bogenhausen" title="Bogenhausen">Bogenhausen</a> (13), <a href="/wiki/Feldmoching-Hasenbergl" title="Feldmoching-Hasenbergl">Feldmoching-Hasenbergl</a> (24), <a href="/wiki/Hadern" title="Hadern">Hadern</a> (20), <a href="/wiki/Laim" title="Laim">Laim</a> (25), <a href="/wiki/Ludwigsvorstadt-Isarvorstadt" title="Ludwigsvorstadt-Isarvorstadt">Ludwigsvorstadt-Isarvorstadt</a> (2), <a href="/wiki/Maxvorstadt" title="Maxvorstadt">Maxvorstadt</a> (3), <a href="/wiki/Milbertshofen-Am_Hart" title="Milbertshofen-Am Hart">Milbertshofen-Am Hart</a> (11), <a href="/wiki/Moosach_(District_of_Munich)" class="mw-redirect" title="Moosach (District of Munich)">Moosach</a> (10), <a href="/wiki/Neuhausen-Nymphenburg" title="Neuhausen-Nymphenburg">Neuhausen-Nymphenburg</a> (9), <a href="/wiki/Obergiesing" title="Obergiesing">Obergiesing</a> (17), <a href="/wiki/Pasing-Obermenzing" title="Pasing-Obermenzing">Pasing-Obermenzing</a> (21), <a href="/wiki/Ramersdorf-Perlach" title="Ramersdorf-Perlach">Ramersdorf-Perlach</a> (16), <a href="/wiki/Schwabing-Freimann" class="mw-redirect" title="Schwabing-Freimann">Schwabing-Freimann</a> (12), <a href="/wiki/Schwabing-West" class="mw-redirect" title="Schwabing-West">Schwabing-West</a> (4), <a href="/wiki/Schwanthalerh%C3%B6he" title="Schwanthalerhöhe">Schwanthalerhöhe</a> (8), <a href="/wiki/Sendling" title="Sendling">Sendling</a> (6), <a href="/wiki/Sendling-Westpark" title="Sendling-Westpark">Sendling-Westpark</a> (7), <a href="/wiki/Thalkirchen-Obersendling-Forstenried-F%C3%BCrstenried-Solln" title="Thalkirchen-Obersendling-Forstenried-Fürstenried-Solln">Thalkirchen-Obersendling-Forstenried-Fürstenried-Solln</a> (19), <a href="/wiki/Trudering-Riem" title="Trudering-Riem">Trudering-Riem</a> (15) and <a href="/wiki/Untergiesing-Harlaching" title="Untergiesing-Harlaching">Untergiesing-Harlaching</a> (18).</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Munich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Munich" title="Architecture of Munich">Architecture of Munich</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rathaus_and_Marienplatz_from_Peterskirche_-_August_2006.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Rathaus_and_Marienplatz_from_Peterskirche_-_August_2006.jpg/220px-Rathaus_and_Marienplatz_from_Peterskirche_-_August_2006.jpg" width="220" height="190" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Rathaus_and_Marienplatz_from_Peterskirche_-_August_2006.jpg/330px-Rathaus_and_Marienplatz_from_Peterskirche_-_August_2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Rathaus_and_Marienplatz_from_Peterskirche_-_August_2006.jpg/440px-Rathaus_and_Marienplatz_from_Peterskirche_-_August_2006.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2089" data-file-height="1800" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rathaus_and_Marienplatz_from_Peterskirche_-_August_2006.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/New_Town_Hall_(Munich)" title="New Town Hall (Munich)">New Town Hall</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marienplatz" title="Marienplatz">Marienplatz</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Frauenkirche_Munich_-_View_from_Peterskirche_Tower2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Frauenkirche_Munich_-_View_from_Peterskirche_Tower2.jpg/220px-Frauenkirche_Munich_-_View_from_Peterskirche_Tower2.jpg" width="220" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Frauenkirche_Munich_-_View_from_Peterskirche_Tower2.jpg/330px-Frauenkirche_Munich_-_View_from_Peterskirche_Tower2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Frauenkirche_Munich_-_View_from_Peterskirche_Tower2.jpg/440px-Frauenkirche_Munich_-_View_from_Peterskirche_Tower2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3952" data-file-height="2464" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Frauenkirche_Munich_-_View_from_Peterskirche_Tower2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Munich_Frauenkirche" title="Munich Frauenkirche">Frauenkirche</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:M%C3%BCnchen,_Viktualienmarkt_met_das_Alte_Rathaus_D-1-62-000-4289_positie2_2012-08-05_15.29.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/M%C3%BCnchen%2C_Viktualienmarkt_met_das_Alte_Rathaus_D-1-62-000-4289_positie2_2012-08-05_15.29.jpg/220px-M%C3%BCnchen%2C_Viktualienmarkt_met_das_Alte_Rathaus_D-1-62-000-4289_positie2_2012-08-05_15.29.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/M%C3%BCnchen%2C_Viktualienmarkt_met_das_Alte_Rathaus_D-1-62-000-4289_positie2_2012-08-05_15.29.jpg/330px-M%C3%BCnchen%2C_Viktualienmarkt_met_das_Alte_Rathaus_D-1-62-000-4289_positie2_2012-08-05_15.29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/M%C3%BCnchen%2C_Viktualienmarkt_met_das_Alte_Rathaus_D-1-62-000-4289_positie2_2012-08-05_15.29.jpg/440px-M%C3%BCnchen%2C_Viktualienmarkt_met_das_Alte_Rathaus_D-1-62-000-4289_positie2_2012-08-05_15.29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4403" data-file-height="3302" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:M%C3%BCnchen,_Viktualienmarkt_met_das_Alte_Rathaus_D-1-62-000-4289_positie2_2012-08-05_15.29.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Viktualienmarkt with the Altes Rathaus</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The city has an eclectic mix of historic and modern architecture, because historic buildings destroyed in World War II were reconstructed, and new landmarks were constructed. A survey by the Society's Centre for Sustainable Destinations for the <a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_Traveller" class="mw-redirect" title="National Geographic Traveller">National Geographic Traveller</a> chose over 100 historic destinations around the world and ranked Munich 30th.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inner_city">Inner city</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Munich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Inner city">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At the centre of the city is the <i><a href="/wiki/Marienplatz" title="Marienplatz">Marienplatz</a></i> – a large open square named after the Mariensäule, a <a href="/wiki/Marian_and_Holy_Trinity_columns" title="Marian and Holy Trinity columns">Marian column</a> in its centre – with the <a href="/wiki/Old_Town_Hall,_Munich" title="Old Town Hall, Munich">Old</a> and the <a href="/wiki/New_Town_Hall,_Munich" class="mw-redirect" title="New Town Hall, Munich">New Town Hall</a>. Its tower contains the <i><a href="/wiki/Rathaus-Glockenspiel" title="Rathaus-Glockenspiel">Rathaus-Glockenspiel</a></i>. Three gates of the demolished medieval fortification survive – the <i><a href="/wiki/Isartor" title="Isartor">Isartor</a></i> in the east, the <i><a href="/wiki/Sendlinger_Tor" title="Sendlinger Tor">Sendlinger Tor</a></i> in the south and the <i><a href="/wiki/Karlstor" title="Karlstor">Karlstor</a></i> in the west of the inner city. The Karlstor leads up to the <i><a href="/wiki/Karlsplatz_(Stachus)" title="Karlsplatz (Stachus)">Stachus</a></i>, a grand square dominated by the <i><a href="/wiki/Justizpalast_(Munich)" title="Justizpalast (Munich)">Justizpalast</a> (Palace of Justice)</i> and a fountain.</p>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/St._Peter%27s_Church,_Munich" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Peter's Church, Munich">Peterskirche</a></i> close to Marienplatz is the oldest church of the inner city. It was first built during the <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque</a> period, and was the focus of the early monastic settlement in Munich before the city's official foundation in 1158. Nearby St. Peter the Gothic hall-church <i><a href="/wiki/Heiliggeistkirche_(Munich)" title="Heiliggeistkirche (Munich)">Heiliggeistkirche</a></i> (The Church of the Holy Spirit) was converted to baroque style from 1724 onwards and looks down upon the <a href="/wiki/Viktualienmarkt" title="Viktualienmarkt">Viktualienmarkt</a>, the most popular market of Munich.</p>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Munich_Frauenkirche" title="Munich Frauenkirche">Frauenkirche</a></i> is the most famous building in the city centre and serves as the cathedral for the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Archdiocese_of_Munich_and_Freising" title="Roman Catholic Archdiocese of Munich and Freising">Archdiocese of Munich and Freising</a>. The nearby <i><a href="/wiki/St._Michael%27s_Church,_Munich" title="St. Michael's Church, Munich">Michaelskirche</a></i> is the largest <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">renaissance</a> church north of the Alps, while the <i><a href="/wiki/Theatine_Church,_Munich" title="Theatine Church, Munich">Theatinerkirche</a></i> is a <a href="/wiki/Basilica" title="Basilica">basilica</a> in Italianate high baroque, which had a major influence on Southern German <a href="/wiki/Baroque" title="Baroque">baroque</a> architecture. Its dome dominates the <a href="/wiki/Odeonsplatz" title="Odeonsplatz">Odeonsplatz</a>. Other baroque churches in the inner city which are worth a detour are the <i><a href="/wiki/B%C3%BCrgersaalkirche_(Munich)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bürgersaalkirche (Munich)">Bürgersaalkirche</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Trinity_Church_(Munich)" title="Trinity Church (Munich)">Dreifaltigkeitskirche</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/St._Anna_Damenstiftskirche_(Munich)" title="St. Anna Damenstiftskirche (Munich)">St. Anna Damenstiftskirche</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Parish_Church_of_St._Anna_(Lehel,_Munich)" title="Parish Church of St. Anna (Lehel, Munich)">St. Anna im Lehel</a></i>, the first <a href="/wiki/Rococo" title="Rococo">rococo</a> church in Bavaria. The <i><a href="/wiki/Asamkirche,_Munich" class="mw-redirect" title="Asamkirche, Munich">Asamkirche</a></i> was endowed and built by the Brothers <a href="/wiki/Cosmas_Damian_Asam" title="Cosmas Damian Asam">Asam</a>, pioneering artists of the rococo period.</p>
<p>The large <i><a href="/wiki/Munich_Residenz" title="Munich Residenz">Residenz</a></i> palace complex (begun in 1385) on the edge of Munich's Old Town, Germany's largest urban palace, ranks among Europe's most significant museums of interior decoration. Having undergone several extensions, it contains also the <a href="/wiki/Schatzkammer" title="Schatzkammer">treasury</a> and the splendid rococo <i><a href="/wiki/Cuvilli%C3%A9s_Theatre" title="Cuvilliés Theatre">Cuvilliés Theatre</a></i>. Next door to the Residenz the neo-classical opera, the <i><a href="/wiki/National_Theatre_Munich" title="National Theatre Munich">National Theatre</a></i> was erected. Among the baroque and neoclassical mansions which still exist in Munich are the <i><a href="/wiki/Palais_Porcia" title="Palais Porcia">Palais Porcia</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Palais_Preysing" title="Palais Preysing">Palais Preysing</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Palais_Holnstein" class="mw-redirect" title="Palais Holnstein">Palais Holnstein</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Prinz-Carl-Palais" title="Prinz-Carl-Palais">Prinz-Carl-Palais</a></i>. All mansions are situated close to the Residenz, same as the <i><a href="/wiki/Alter_Hof" title="Alter Hof">Alte Hof</a></i>, a medieval castle and first residence of the Wittelsbach dukes in Munich.</p>
<p>Lehel, a bourgeoise quarter east of the Altstadt, is characterised by countless well-preserved (and in parts excellently reconstructed) town houses, giving a thorough impression of the "old Munich" outside of the main tourist routes. <i><a href="/wiki/St._Luke%27s_Church,_Munich" title="St. Luke's Church, Munich">St. Lukas</a></i> is the largest Protestant Church in Munich.</p>
<p>The inner city has been recreated<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> in the virtual world of <a href="/wiki/Second_Life" title="Second Life">Second Life</a> and can be visited for a virtual sight seeing tour.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Royal_avenues_and_squares">Royal avenues and squares</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Munich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Royal avenues and squares">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ludwigstra%C3%9FeMUC.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Ludwigstra%C3%9FeMUC.jpg/220px-Ludwigstra%C3%9FeMUC.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Ludwigstra%C3%9FeMUC.jpg/330px-Ludwigstra%C3%9FeMUC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Ludwigstra%C3%9FeMUC.jpg/440px-Ludwigstra%C3%9FeMUC.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="2304" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ludwigstra%C3%9FeMUC.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ludwigstra%C3%9Fe_(Munich)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ludwigstraße (Munich)">Ludwigstrasse</a> from above, <a href="/wiki/Highlight_Towers" title="Highlight Towers">Highlight Towers</a> in the background</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Four grand royal avenues of the 19th century with magnificent official buildings connect Munich's inner city with its then-suburbs:</p>
<p>The neoclassical <i><a href="/wiki/Brienner_Stra%C3%9Fe_(Munich)" title="Brienner Straße (Munich)">Briennerstrasse</a></i>, starting at <i><a href="/wiki/Odeonsplatz" title="Odeonsplatz">Odeonsplatz</a></i> on the northern fringe of the Old Town close to the Residenz, runs from east to west and opens into the impressive <i><a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6nigsplatz,_Munich" title="Königsplatz, Munich">Königsplatz</a></i>, designed with the "<a href="/wiki/Doric_order" title="Doric order">Doric</a>" <i><a href="/wiki/Propylaea_(Munich)" title="Propylaea (Munich)">Propyläen</a></i>, the "<a href="/wiki/Ionic_order" title="Ionic order">Ionic</a>" <i><a href="/wiki/Glyptothek" title="Glyptothek">Glyptothek</a></i> and the "<a href="/wiki/Corinthian_order" title="Corinthian order">Corinthian</a>" <i><a href="/wiki/Staatliche_Antikensammlungen" title="Staatliche Antikensammlungen">State Museum of Classical Art</a></i>, on its back side <a href="/wiki/St._Boniface%27s_Abbey,_Munich" title="St. Boniface's Abbey, Munich">St. Boniface's Abbey</a> was erected. The area around Königsplatz is home to the <a href="/wiki/Kunstareal" title="Kunstareal">Kunstareal</a>, Munich's gallery and museum quarter (as described <a href="/wiki/Munich#Museums" title="Munich">below</a>).</p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Ludwigstra%C3%9Fe_(Munich)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ludwigstraße (Munich)">Ludwigstrasse</a></i> also begins at <i><a href="/wiki/Odeonsplatz" title="Odeonsplatz">Odeonsplatz</a></i> and runs from south to north, skirting the <i><a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Maximilian_University_of_Munich" title="Ludwig Maximilian University of Munich">Ludwig-Maximilians-Universität</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Ludwigskirche_(Munich)" title="Ludwigskirche (Munich)">St. Louis church</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Bavarian_State_Library" title="Bavarian State Library">Bavarian State Library</a></i> and numerous state ministries and palaces. The southern part of the avenue was constructed in Italian renaissance style, while the north is strongly influenced by Italian Romanesque architecture. The <a href="/wiki/Siegestor" title="Siegestor">Siegestor</a> (gate of victory) sits at the northern end of Ludwigstraße, where the latter passes over into Leopoldstraße and the district of <a href="/wiki/Schwabing" title="Schwabing">Schwabing</a> begins.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Maximilianeum_Building.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/The_Maximilianeum_Building.jpg/220px-The_Maximilianeum_Building.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/The_Maximilianeum_Building.jpg/330px-The_Maximilianeum_Building.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/The_Maximilianeum_Building.jpg/440px-The_Maximilianeum_Building.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1021" data-file-height="680" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Maximilianeum_Building.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Maximilianeum" title="Maximilianeum">Maximilianeum</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The neo-Gothic <i><a href="/wiki/Maximilianstra%C3%9Fe_(Munich)" title="Maximilianstraße (Munich)">Maximilianstraße</a></i> starts at <i><a href="/wiki/Max-Joseph-Platz" title="Max-Joseph-Platz">Max-Joseph-Platz</a></i>, where the <i>Residenz</i> and the National Theatre are situated, and runs from west to east. The avenue is framed by elaborately structured neo-Gothic buildings which house, among others, the <i><a href="/wiki/Munich_Kammerspiele" title="Munich Kammerspiele">Schauspielhaus</a></i>, the <i>Building of the district government of Upper Bavaria</i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/State_Museum_of_Ethnology" class="mw-redirect" title="State Museum of Ethnology">Museum of Ethnology</a></i>. After crossing the river Isar, the avenue circles the <i><a href="/wiki/Maximilianeum" title="Maximilianeum">Maximilianeum</a></i>, home of the <a href="/wiki/Landtag_of_Bavaria" title="Landtag of Bavaria">state parliament</a>. The western portion of Maximilianstraße is known for its designer shops, luxury boutiques, jewellery stores, and one of Munich's foremost five-star hotels, the <i>Hotel Vier Jahreszeiten</i>.</p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Prinzregentenstra%C3%9Fe_(Munich)" title="Prinzregentenstraße (Munich)">Prinzregentenstrasse</a></i> runs parallel to Maximilianstraße and begins at <i><a href="/wiki/Prinz-Carl-Palais" title="Prinz-Carl-Palais">Prinz-Carl-Palais</a></i>. Many museums can be found along the avenue, such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Haus_der_Kunst" title="Haus der Kunst">Haus der Kunst</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Bavarian_National_Museum" title="Bavarian National Museum">Bavarian National Museum</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Schackgalerie" title="Schackgalerie">Schackgalerie</a></i>. The avenue crosses the Isar and circles the <i>Friedensengel monument</i>, then passing the <i><a href="/wiki/Villa_Stuck" title="Villa Stuck">Villa Stuck</a></i> and Hitler's old apartment. The <i><a href="/wiki/Prinzregententheater" title="Prinzregententheater">Prinzregententheater</a></i> is at Prinzregentenplatz further to the east.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_boroughs">Other boroughs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Munich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Other boroughs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Schwabing" title="Schwabing">Schwabing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maxvorstadt" title="Maxvorstadt">Maxvorstadt</a>, many beautiful streets with continuous rows of <a href="/wiki/Gr%C3%BCnderzeit" title="Gründerzeit">Gründerzeit</a> buildings can be found. Rows of elegant town houses and spectacular urban palais in many colours, often elaborately decorated with ornamental details on their façades, make up large parts of the areas west of <a href="/wiki/Leopoldstra%C3%9Fe" class="mw-redirect" title="Leopoldstraße">Leopoldstraße</a> (Schwabing's main shopping street), while in the eastern areas between Leopoldstraße and <a href="/wiki/Englischer_Garten" title="Englischer Garten">Englischer Garten</a> similar buildings alternate with almost rural-looking houses and whimsical mini-castles, often decorated with small towers. Numerous tiny alleys and shady lanes connect the larger streets and little plazas of the area, conveying the legendary artist's quarter's flair and atmosphere convincingly like it was at the turn of the 20th century. The wealthy district of <a href="/wiki/Bogenhausen" title="Bogenhausen">Bogenhausen</a> in the east of Munich is another little-known area (at least among tourists) rich in extravagant architecture, especially around Prinzregentenstraße. One of Bogenhausen's most beautiful buildings is <a href="/wiki/Villa_Stuck" title="Villa Stuck">Villa Stuck</a>, famed residence of painter <a href="/wiki/Franz_von_Stuck" class="mw-redirect" title="Franz von Stuck">Franz von Stuck</a>.</p>
<p>Two large baroque palaces in <a href="/wiki/Nymphenburg" class="mw-redirect" title="Nymphenburg">Nymphenburg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oberschleissheim" class="mw-redirect" title="Oberschleissheim">Oberschleissheim</a> are reminders of Bavaria's royal past. <i>Schloss Nymphenburg</i> (<a href="/wiki/Nymphenburg_Palace" title="Nymphenburg Palace">Nymphenburg Palace</a>), some 6&#160;km (4&#160;mi) north west of the city centre, is surrounded by an impressive park and is considered to be one of Europe's most beautiful royal residences. 2&#160;km (1&#160;mi) northwest of Nymphenburg Palace is <i>Schloss Blutenburg</i> (<a href="/wiki/Blutenburg_Castle" title="Blutenburg Castle">Blutenburg Castle</a>), an old ducal country seat with a late-Gothic palace church. <i>Schloss Fürstenried</i> (<a href="/wiki/F%C3%BCrstenried_Palace" title="Fürstenried Palace">Fürstenried Palace</a>), a baroque palace of similar structure to Nymphenburg but of much smaller size, was erected around the same time in the south west of Munich. The second large baroque residence is <i>Schloss Schleissheim</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Schleissheim_Palace" title="Schleissheim Palace">Schleissheim Palace</a></i>), located in the suburb of <a href="/wiki/Oberschlei%C3%9Fheim" title="Oberschleißheim">Oberschleissheim</a>, a palace complex encompassing three separate residences: <i>Altes Schloss Schleissheim</i> (the old palace), <i>Neues Schloss Schleissheim</i> (the new palace) and <i>Schloss Lustheim</i> (Lustheim Palace). Most parts of the palace complex serve as museums and art galleries. <a href="/wiki/Deutsches_Museum" title="Deutsches Museum">Deutsches Museum</a>'s <i>Flugwerft Schleissheim</i> flight exhibition centre is located nearby, on the Schleissheim Special Landing Field. The <a href="/wiki/Bavaria_statue" title="Bavaria statue">Bavaria statue</a> before the neo-classical <a href="/wiki/Ruhmeshalle_(Munich)" title="Ruhmeshalle (Munich)">Ruhmeshalle</a> is a monumental, bronze sand-cast 19th-century statue at <a href="/wiki/Theresienwiese" title="Theresienwiese">Theresienwiese</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Gr%C3%BCnwald_castle" class="mw-redirect" title="Grünwald castle">Grünwald castle</a> is the only medieval castle in the Munich area which still exists.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Image-Schloss_Nymphenburg_Munich_CC_edit3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Image-Schloss_Nymphenburg_Munich_CC_edit3.jpg/220px-Image-Schloss_Nymphenburg_Munich_CC_edit3.jpg" width="220" height="80" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Image-Schloss_Nymphenburg_Munich_CC_edit3.jpg/330px-Image-Schloss_Nymphenburg_Munich_CC_edit3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Image-Schloss_Nymphenburg_Munich_CC_edit3.jpg/440px-Image-Schloss_Nymphenburg_Munich_CC_edit3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4921" data-file-height="1796" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Image-Schloss_Nymphenburg_Munich_CC_edit3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Nymphenburg_Palace" title="Nymphenburg Palace">Nymphenburg Palace</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BMW-HQ.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/BMW-HQ.jpg/170px-BMW-HQ.jpg" width="170" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/BMW-HQ.jpg/255px-BMW-HQ.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/BMW-HQ.jpg/340px-BMW-HQ.jpg 2x" data-file-width="731" data-file-height="973" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BMW-HQ.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/BMW" title="BMW">BMW</a> Headquarters</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/St_Michael_in_Berg_am_Laim_(Munich)" title="St Michael in Berg am Laim (Munich)">St Michael in Berg am Laim</a></i> might be the most remarkable church in the suburbs. Another church of <a href="/wiki/Johann_Michael_Fischer" title="Johann Michael Fischer">Johann Michael Fischer</a> is <i>St George in Bogenhausen</i>. Most of the boroughs have parish churches which originate from the Middle Ages like the most famous church of pilgrimage in Munich <i><a href="/wiki/St._Mary%27s_Church,_Ramersdorf" title="St. Mary's Church, Ramersdorf">St Mary in Ramersdorf</a></i>. The oldest church within the city borders is <i><a href="/wiki/Holy_Cross_Church,_Munich" title="Holy Cross Church, Munich">Heilig Kreuz</a></i> in Fröttmaning next to the Allianz-Arena, known for its Romanesque fresco. Especially in its suburbs, Munich features a wide and diverse array of modern architecture, although strict culturally sensitive height limitations for buildings have limited the construction of skyscrapers to avoid a loss of views to the distant Bavarian Alps. Most high-rise buildings are clustered at the northern edge of Munich in the skyline, like the <i><a href="/wiki/Hypo-Haus" title="Hypo-Haus">Hypo-Haus</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Arabella_High-Rise_Building" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabella High-Rise Building">Arabella High-Rise Building</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Highlight_Towers" title="Highlight Towers">Highlight Towers</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Uptown_Munich" class="mw-redirect" title="Uptown Munich">Uptown Munich</a></i>, <i>Münchner Tor</i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/BMW_Headquarters" title="BMW Headquarters">BMW Headquarters</a></i> next to the Olympic Park. Several other high-rise buildings are located near the city centre and on the <a href="/wiki/Siemens" title="Siemens">Siemens</a> campus in southern Munich. A landmark of modern Munich is also the architecture of the sport stadiums (as described <a href="/wiki/Munich#Sports" title="Munich">below</a>).</p>
<p>In Fasangarten is the former <a href="/wiki/McGraw_Kaserne" title="McGraw Kaserne">McGraw Kaserne</a>, a former US army base, near <a href="/wiki/Stadelheim_Prison" title="Stadelheim Prison">Stadelheim Prison</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parks">Parks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Munich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Parks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:M%C3%BCnchen_Hofgartentempel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/M%C3%BCnchen_Hofgartentempel.jpg/220px-M%C3%BCnchen_Hofgartentempel.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/M%C3%BCnchen_Hofgartentempel.jpg/330px-M%C3%BCnchen_Hofgartentempel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/M%C3%BCnchen_Hofgartentempel.jpg/440px-M%C3%BCnchen_Hofgartentempel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2175" data-file-height="1631" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:M%C3%BCnchen_Hofgartentempel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hofgarten with the dome of the state chancellery near the <a href="/wiki/Munich_Residenz" title="Munich Residenz">Residenz</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Munich is a densely-built city but still offers numerous public parks. The <i><a href="/wiki/Englischer_Garten" title="Englischer Garten">Englischer Garten</a></i>, close to the city centre and covering an area of 3.7&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (1.4&#160;sq&#160;mi) (larger than Central Park in New York), is one of the world's largest urban public parks. It contains a famous <a href="/wiki/Naturism" title="Naturism">nudist</a> area, numerous bicycle and jogging tracks as well as bridle-paths. It is considered the "green lung" of Munich and one of the city's best-loved features. It was designed and laid out by <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Thompson" title="Benjamin Thompson">Benjamin Thompson</a>, Count of Rumford, for both pleasure and as a work area for the city's vagrants and homeless. Nowadays it is entirely a park, its southern half being dominated by wide and extremely well-kept open areas, hills, monuments and beach-like stretches (along the streams Eisbach and Schwabinger Bach), which get crowded in summer. In contrast, its less-frequented northern part is much more quiet, idyllic and natural-seeming, at times resembling a natural preserve more than an urban public park: it has lots of old trees, thick undergrowth, winding streams, hidden meadows and is pervaded by numerous romantic pathways. Multiple <a href="/wiki/Beer_garden" title="Beer garden">Biergartens</a> can be found in both parts of the Englischer Garten, the most well known being located at the <a href="/wiki/Englischer_Garten#Chinesischer_Turm" title="Englischer Garten">Chinese Pagoda</a>.</p>
<p>Other large green spaces are the modern <i><a href="/wiki/Olympiapark,_Munich" class="mw-redirect" title="Olympiapark, Munich">Olympiapark</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Westpark_(Munich)" title="Westpark (Munich)">Westpark</a></i>, and the parks of <a href="/wiki/Nymphenburg_Palace" title="Nymphenburg Palace">Nymphenburg Palace</a> (with the <a href="/wiki/Botanischer_Garten_M%C3%BCnchen-Nymphenburg" title="Botanischer Garten München-Nymphenburg">Botanischer Garten München-Nymphenburg</a> to the north), and <a href="/wiki/Schleissheim_Palace" title="Schleissheim Palace">Schleissheim Palace</a>. The city's oldest park is the <i><a href="/wiki/Hofgarten_(Munich)" title="Hofgarten (Munich)">Hofgarten</a></i>, near the Residenz, dating back to the 16th century. Best known for the largest beergarden in town is the former royal <i>Hirschgarten</i>, founded in 1780 for deer, which still live there.</p>
<p>The city's <a href="/wiki/Zoo" title="Zoo">zoo</a> is the <i><a href="/wiki/Tierpark_Hellabrunn" class="mw-redirect" title="Tierpark Hellabrunn">Tierpark Hellabrunn</a></i> near the Flaucher Island in the Isar in the south of the city. Another notable park is <a href="/wiki/Ostpark_(Munich)" title="Ostpark (Munich)">Ostpark</a> located in the <a href="/wiki/Ramersdorf-Perlach" title="Ramersdorf-Perlach">Ramersdorf-Perlach</a> borough which also houses the <i>Michaelibad</i>, the largest waterpark in Munich.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">Sports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Munich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Sports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sports_in_Munich" class="mw-redirect" title="Sports in Munich">Sports in Munich</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Allianz_Arena_zu_verschiedenen_Zeiten.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Allianz_Arena_zu_verschiedenen_Zeiten.jpg/220px-Allianz_Arena_zu_verschiedenen_Zeiten.jpg" width="220" height="203" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Allianz_Arena_zu_verschiedenen_Zeiten.jpg/330px-Allianz_Arena_zu_verschiedenen_Zeiten.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Allianz_Arena_zu_verschiedenen_Zeiten.jpg/440px-Allianz_Arena_zu_verschiedenen_Zeiten.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4811" data-file-height="4434" /></a>
